(Paper)
<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2018, <strong>10</strong>, 6671-6677</div><h1 id="sect178"><span class="title_heading">A stable layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth nanocomposite as a long-life and high-rate cathode for sodium-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Peiyu 
            Hou
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Peiyu&nbsp;Hou (orcid.org/0000-0003-0476-5812) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-0476-5812"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Jiangmei 
            Yin
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Xianhang 
            Lu
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Jiaming 
            Li
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Yue 
            Zhao
          
        
      </span><span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Xijin 
            Xu
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Xijin&nbsp;Xu (orcid.org/0000-0002-3877-6483) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-3877-6483"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*
      <br><span class="italic">School of Physics and Technology, University of Jinan, Jinan, 250022, Shandong Province, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:sps_xjxu@ujn.edu.cn">sps_xjxu@ujn.edu.cn</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      23rd January 2018
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 27th February 2018</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 28th February 2018</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Layered sodium transition-metal oxides, Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small>, with large theoretical capacity are regarded as an important class of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, they usually exhibit inferior thermodynamic stability and sluggish Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> kinetics due to the unwanted phase transitions and large Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>-ionic radius. In this work, considering the beneficial synergistic effects of layered P2/P3 and <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel phases, a stable layered/spinel intergrowth nanocomposite Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is rationally designed and successfully prepared <span class="italic">via</span> a co-precipitation route and a subsequent solid-state reaction, and the triphase synergy in this layered/spinel nanocomposite is demonstrated. In Na/Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]O<small><sub>2</sub></small> half-cells, it delivers a high specific capacity of 180 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> and a good cycling stability, with a capacity retention of 87.6% after 100 cycles, at a rate of 0.1C between 1.5 and 4.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>). The large reversible capacity of 105 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> is also achieved even at a high rate of 10C, indicating high-rate capability. Besides, the full-cells using this nanocomposite as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode exhibit long-term cycle-life and high-power properties, indicating the expected merits of layered/spinel mixed phases. The superior sodium storage performance of this layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth nanocomposite makes it a promising candidate as a long-life and high-rate cathode for SIBs.</p></div><hr>
    
      <h2 id="sect254"><span class="a_heading">Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>The transition from traditional fossil fuels to renewable energy, particularly clean solar energy, necessarily needs large-scale electrical energy storage (EES).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with high gravimetric/volumetric energy-density are widely applied in portable electronic devices and their application is gradually being expanded to electrical vehicles (EVs).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> But due to their low abundance in the Earth's crust, 20 ppm, lithium sources are not sufficient to satisfy the increasing demands of LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9</span></sup></a> Therefore the necessity to substitute lithium with cheaper alternatives has recently attracted considerable attention to develop low-cost rechargeable batteries. In contrast to lithium, sodium is one of the most abundant elements and exhibits similar chemical properties to lithium, indicating that sodium-ion chemistries could be utilized in a similar battery system, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2,1015</span></sup></a></span>
      <p class="otherpara">Similar to LIBs, the chemical potential difference of the sodium ions between anode and cathode electrodes creates a voltage in the SIBs. And the electrochemical properties are highly related to the electrode materials used in batteries. Among these electrodes, layered sodium transition-metal oxides, Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Me = Ni, Co, Mn, <span class="italic">etc</span>.), with a large theoretical specific capacity (up to 200 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) are regarded as a significant class of cathode materials for SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1625</span></sup></a> Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> exist as one of several polytypes according to the varied stacking of the close-packed oxygen layers. Following the systematization proposed by Delmas <span class="italic">et al.</span>, three main groups are defined: P2 (ABBA stacking), O3 (ABCABC stacking), and P3 (ABBCCA stacking).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> The sodium ions occupy various coordination environments (P = prismatic and O = octahedral) depending on the class of the polytype.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To date, many P2- and O3-type Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> have been reported as cathode candidates for SIBs. Especially in the P2-type structure, sodium ions can diffuse directly between two face-sharing sites, indicating better rate capabilities, which has attracted considerable attention for high-rate electrodes. However, since the prismatic sites with large sodium ions are energetically stable, the over-extraction of sodium ions generally induces the gliding of MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs along with the generation of octahedral sites.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> As a result, a new O2-type structure with AB AC AB oxygen packing forms, which leads to an irreversible structural collapse and rapid capacity degradation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> Cation substitution, especially Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Mg<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, as a main structural regulation approach, is generally used to suppress the unwanted phase transition of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MeO<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2832</span></sup></a> Besides the P2-type structure, the O3-type polymorphs are sufficient sodium-ion reservoirs, and can offer sufficient sodium, especially in full SIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> But for this O3-type electrode, the diffusion of sodium ions from one octahedral site to another must occur through the edge sharing tetrahedral site between them.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> This intermediate site should impose a high energy barrier for facile sodium ion diffusion. Therefore, the phase transformation and the sluggish kinetics are inevitable, limiting the electrochemical properties.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3537</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Recently, multiple-phase hybrid composites were developed and they exhibited superior electrochemical performance compared with the pure P2-, P3-, or O3-type layered compounds.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3846</span></sup></a> Passerini <span class="italic">et al.</span> have prepared a layered P3/P2 hybrid Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.22</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.12</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.66</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small>, but it exhibited dissatisfactory cycle-life and rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> Afterwards, they have further introduced an ionic liquid-based electrolyte to enhance the electrochemical performance by reducing Mn<small><sup>2+</sup></small> dissolution.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> Zhou and co-workers have also prepared a stable P3/P2-biphasic Na<small><sub>0.66</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">via</span> a facile solgel route, and it revealed enhanced cycling stability and rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Johnson <span class="italic">et al.</span> have reported layered P2/O3 intergrowth Na<small><sub>1<span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<span class="italic">x</span>  0.3) as a high-rate cathode for SIBs, in which the cycle-formed P2/P3-intergrowth phases facilitate the smooth Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> Lately, a spinel-integrated P2-type layered composite Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>1/6</sub></small>Co<small><sub>1/6</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>2/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> as a high-rate cathode for SIBs was reported by Manthiram <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> It was believed that the integrated-spinel phase in the P2-type phase could accelerate fast electron transfer to coordinate with the timely Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion/extraction. In this work, considering the beneficial synergistic effects of layered P3/P2 phases and <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel phase, a stable layered P2/P3 and <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel intergrowth composite Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small> (LS-NCM) is rationally designed, and the triphase synergy in this layered P2/P3 and spinel nanocomposite is demonstrated for the first time. Both in half-cell and full-cell configurations, superior sodium-ion storage performance is achieved by using this layered/spinel intergrowth nanocomposite as a cathode, which makes it a promising candidate as a long-life and high-rate cathode for SIBs.</p>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect335"><span class="a_heading">Experimental</span></h2>
      
        <h3 id="sect337"><span class="b_heading">Preparation of the target precursors and cathodes</span></h3>
        <span>To synthesize the target precursors, stoichiometric NiSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>6H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, CoSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>7H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O, and MnSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O (cationic ratio of NiCoMn = 2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1.5<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">6.5) were dissolved in 1 L deionized water to form a 2 M starting solution, which was further dropped into a continuously stirred tank reactor (CSTR, capacity of 10 L). Meanwhile, 4 M Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> solution (aq.) as a pH control agent and 0.2 M ammonia solution (aq.) as a ligand were also dropped into the CSTR, achieving the target precursor [Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]CO<small><sub>3</sub></small>. Then, the obtained precursors were mixed with a stoichiometric ratio of NaOH (Na/M = 0.5) and the mixture was heated to 800 C for 10 h in air to form the layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth composite.</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect352"><span class="b_heading">Materials characterization</span></h3>
        <span>X-ray diffraction (XRD, D/MAX-2500, Rigaku) was used to study the phase structure. The morphology and elemental mapping were analyzed using a scanning electron microscope (SEM, FEG250, FEI QUANTA) equipped with an energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS). The atomic fractions of NiCoMn of these precursors were measured using an inductively coupled plasma spectrometer (ICP, SPS 7800, Seiko Instruments). The chemical states of the layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth composite were measured by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Escalab250).</span>
      
      
        <h3 id="sect355"><span class="b_heading">Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>Positive electrodes composed of 80 wt% cathode materials, 10 wt% PVdF, and 10 wt% Super P are first prepared, which is the same as that described in our previous work.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47,48</span></sup></a> The positive electrode disks with similar active cathode loading (2.02.5 mg cm<small><sup>2</sup></small>) were utilized to assemble 2032-type half-cells and full-cells. In sodium-ion half-cells, the pure sodium metal was employed as the negative electrode, and 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used as the electrolyte. Galvanostatic chargedischarge tests were conducted between 1.5 and 4.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) through a LAND instrument (CT-2001A). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was conducted on an electrochemical workstation (CHI660e, Chenhua) with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>. All the measurements were performed at room temperature (25 C  2 C).</span>
      
    
    
      <h2 id="sect364"><span class="a_heading">Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>The desired precursor [Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> for subsequent solid-state reaction with sodium was first synthesized using a previously developed co-precipitation route.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49,50</span></sup></a> The resulting precursor appears to be monodispersed spherical secondary particles with an average size of 12.62 m (Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) that are assembled by nano-sized primary grains as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(ac)</a>. The XRD results for the products confirm that the as-prepared precursors are indexed to a layered MnCO<small><sub>3</sub></small> structure with the <span class="italic">R</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">c</span> group,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit51"><sup><span class="sup_ref">51,52</span></sup></a> and measurable impurity is not observed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(d)</a>. The sharp diffraction peaks, such as (012), (104), (113), <span class="italic">etc</span>., indicate the good crystallinity of these precursors prepared by carbonate co-precipitation reactions. In addition, ICP measurements further demonstrate that the actual molar ratio of NiCoMn (0.195/0.153/0.652) in the as-prepared precursors is in agreement with the target value of 0.2/0.15/0.65.</span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(ac) SEM images and (d) XRD patterns of the as-prepared carbonate precursors [Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]CO<small><sub>3</sub></small> through a carbonate co-precipitation route.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To prepare the layered P3/P2 and <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel intergrowth composite, these precursors are mixed with a stoichiometric ratio (Na/Me = 0.5) of sodium hydroxide and the mixture is heated to 800 C in a muffle furnace. The multi-phase structure of this composite is analyzed by XRD, and the obtained diffraction results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2</a>. It is obvious that most of the diffraction peaks of the XRD pattern can be well indexed to a hexagonal lattice with the space group <span class="italic">R</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span>, which is isostructural with the P3-type (JCPDS: 54-0839) phase. Residual diffraction peaks belong to the hexagonal P2-type phase with the space group <span class="italic">P</span>6<small><sub>3</sub></small>/<span class="italic">mmc</span> (JCPDS: 54-0894) and the cubic spinel phase with the space group <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> (JCPDS: 65-3105). These results confirm the formation of the designed layered P3/P2 and spinel composite. P3/P2-type biphasic layered sodium oxides possess the advantage of superior structural stabilization.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> Besides, the multi-transition-metal spinel oxides Ni<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Co<small><sub><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>Mn<small><sub>3<span class="italic">x</span><span class="italic">y</span></sub></small>O<small><sub>4</sub></small> have high electronic conductivities, reaching orders of 10<small><sup>3</sup></small>10<small><sup>4</sup></small> S cm<small><sup>1</sup></small> at room temperature.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit53"><sup><span class="sup_ref">53,54</span></sup></a> In this regard, it is reasonable that the integration of the <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel phase into the P3/P2-type biphasic phases is capable of significantly improving the electronic/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficient of layered sodium transition-metal oxide cathodes.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">XRD pattern results of the as-prepared layered P3/P2 and spinel composite.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The SEM images and EDS-mapping of the layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth composite after lithiation are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3</a>. It is clear that this composite still maintains a quasi-spherical morphology, but cracks are formed in a part of the secondary particles. The primary grains show polyhedron-like nano-sized particles with a size of 200400 nm, which will shorten the path for Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> migration and electron transfer, indicating good rate capability. Elemental mapping proves the homogeneous distribution of Na, Ni, Co, Mn and O atoms, indicating the layered/spinel nanocomposite for primary grains. From XPS results shown in Fig. S2,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the binding energy of Na 1s locates at 1071.9 eV. The Mn 2p3/2 peaks at 641.7 eV and 642.9 eV are ascribed to Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small> and Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> The binding energies of Ni 2p3/2 locate at 854.6 eV and 855.6 eV, indicating mixed valence states of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> for nickel atoms.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> The binding energy of Co 2p3/2 (781 eV) corresponds to the Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small> state in the layered P3/P2 and spinel nanocomposite.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit52"><sup><span class="sup_ref">52</span></sup></a></p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Low magnification SEM of the micron-sized secondary particles; (b) SEM image of a single spherical particle as well as the corresponding elemental mapping; (c) high magnification SEM of the nano-scaled primary grains.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">The redox couples involved in this LS-NCM electrode are investigated by CVs shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(a)</a>. One oxidation peak at 3.73 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) is observed in the initial anodic reaction. Subsequently, in the following cathodic reaction, two main peaks appeared at 3.58 V and 1.83 V and another weak peak at 2.26 V was detected. Note that the oxidation peak at 3.73 V moves to a lower potential while the reduction peak at 1.83 V shifts to a high value during the second cycle. The peaks located at potentials lower than 3.0 V are related to a Mn<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/Mn<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox process, while the potentials higher than 3.0 V are mainly contributed from Ni<small><sup>2+/3+/4+</sup></small> and Co<small><sup>3+/4+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit55"><sup><span class="sup_ref">55,56</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(be)</a> reveal the initial two charge/discharge curves, and cycling stability of the LS-NCM electrode. The LS-NCM electrode delivers a large reversible capacity of 177.6 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1C as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(b)</a>. As anticipated, it exhibits superior Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation/deintercalation stability. Namely, a high capacity of 155.6 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> with a good capacity retention of 87.6% is achieved after 100 cycles at 0.1C. Nevertheless, continuous cycling curves shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(d)</a> indicate that this electrode shows to some extent electrochemical polarization, especially in charging processes. To further evaluate the structural stability of the LS-NCM electrode that resulted from the layered/spinel mixed phases, similar cycling tests at a high rate of 1C are conducted as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4(e)</a>. These results confirm that a superior capacity retention is observed for the LS-NCM electrode even after 400 cycles, indicating that the layered P3/P2 and spinel mixed phases produce the enhanced structural stability. The systematic comparison of this work <span class="italic">versus</span> recent literature<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38,4042,45,57,58</span></sup></a> with similar material systems is summarized in Table S1.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Among these publications, this LS-NCM electrode shows the best rate capability in the half-cell configuration.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CVs of the LS-NCM electrode at a rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>1</sup></small>; (b) the initial two charge/discharge curves, (c) cycle-life, and (d) varied charge/discharge curves from 5th to 100th cycles of the LS-NCM electrode at 0.1C and 25 C; (e) long-term cycling stability of the LS-NCM electrode at 1C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">High power density is also critical for the applications of rechargeable batteries in large-scale EES.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit59"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5961</span></sup></a> The rate capability of the LS-NCM electrode was determined by increasing the charge/discharge rates from 0.1 to 10C, and the testing results are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>. It exhibits similar reversible capacities (175 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) from 0.1 to 0.5C, while large reversible capacities of 162, 152, 129, and 105 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> are achieved at the high rates of 1, 2, 5, and 10C, respectively. Note that even at the high rate of 10C (the charge/discharge times are only 6 min), the delivered capacity corresponds to a 60% capacity retention of 0.1C. Increased electrochemical polarization is observed for the LS-NCM electrode, especially in the charging processes, as the current density is increased from 0.1 to 10C as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(b)</a>.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Reversible capacity and (b) the corresponding charge/discharge curves of the LS-NCM electrode from 0.1C to 10C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">It is well known that the cycling stability of layered P-type and O-type cathodes is directly related to the phase transition.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42,46,62</span></sup></a> To study the structural evolutions of this layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth cathode, <span class="italic">ex situ</span> XRD is employed at different states of charge and discharge during Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion/extraction processes, as exhibited in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>. It is found that no new diffraction peak beyond the layered P3/P2 and spinel phases is detected except for the shift of peak positions shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(b)</a>, especially for the selected magnified patterns in the ranges of 1417 and 3340. Note that the (311) diffraction peak of the spinel phase moves to a higher angle during the discharge process, indicating the electrochemical activity of this <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel phase, which probably corresponds to the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion. The three-dimensional Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion path of the spinel structure will improve the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> kinetics of this hybrid cathode during redox reactions. Besides, at the end of the charge process, the <span class="italic">c</span> parameter shrinks as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(c)</a>, which was also observed in other layered lithium/sodium metal oxides.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit63"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6365</span></sup></a> It was proposed that the formation and progressive increase in the concentration of local stacking faults resulted in a decrease in the interlayer distance. The stacking faults are formed by the gliding of MO<small><sub>2</sub></small> slabs along the <span class="italic">a</span><span class="italic">b</span> plane, in order to avoid oxygenoxygen interactions at high sodium vacancy concentrations and to maintain a close-packed oxygen arrangement (O2 or O3 structure). In the following discharge process, the <span class="italic">c</span> parameter further decreases and then increases, and finally recovers to the initial state. Apparently, these results demonstrate the structural stability of this layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth composite upon cycling.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">
          <span class="italic">Ex situ</span> XRD patterns collected during the initial charge/discharge process of the LS-NCM electrode between 1.5 and 4.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Sodium ion full cells using LS-NCM as the cathode and hard carbon as the anode (LS-NCM||1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>/EC + DEC|| hard carbon) are assembled to evaluate their electrochemical behaviors. It should be noted that the hard carbon anode was pre-cycled in the half cells to reach a stable platform prior to the fabrication of full cells, as depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(a)</a>. Since the capacity ratio of the anode/cathode reaches 1.5, the specific capacities of full cells are calculated on account of the LS-NCM cathode. The initial charge/discharge curves of the full-cells are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(b)</a>, where the cells are shown to deliver a reversible capacity of around 100 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at 0.1C with nearly 100% initial Coulomb efficiency and an average voltage of 3.0 V. The full-cells exhibit an outstanding cycling stability, maintaining the capacity retention of 90.6% even after 400 cycles at 0.5C as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(c and d)</a>. Besides, the full-cells deliver reversible capacities of 65 and 56 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small> at high rates of 5 and 10C as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(e and f)</a>, respectively, indicating good rate properties. SEM and XRD are employed to evaluate the structural stability of this nanocomposite cathode after 400 cycles at 0.5C in full-cells. The corresponding results are revealed in Fig. S3,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> in which the spherical nanocomposite is maintained after long cycles. In addition, apart from the diffraction peaks of graphite and Al foil, only layered P2/P3 and spinel phases are detected, indicating the absence of unwanted phase transition. These results indicate the superior structural stability of this layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth nanocomposite as a cathode material during repeated charge/discharge cycles.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr00650d-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr00650d/c8nr00650d-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The initial two discharge/charge curves of hard carbon anode at 0.1C (30 mA g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) in the voltage range of 0.013.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>). The electrochemical performance of the LS-NCM1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small>/EC + DEC hard carbon sodium-ion full battery in the potential range of 1.54.0 V: (b) the initial two discharge/charge curves at 0.1C (1C = 100 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>), (c) the cycling stability at 0.5C, (d) changes of discharge/charge curves from the 5th to the 400th cycle, and (e, f) rate properties from 0.1C to 10C.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      <h2 id="sect498"><span class="a_heading">Conclusions</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, layered P3/P2 and <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> spinel mixed phases are rationally designed to improve the thermodynamic stability and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> kinetics of layered sodium transition-metal oxides. A stable layered/spinel intergrowth composite Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>[Ni<small><sub>0.2</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.15</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>0.65</sub></small>]O<small><sub>2</sub></small> is successfully prepared. As expected, the triphase synergy in this layered P2/P3 and spinel nanocomposite is confirmed. In Na/LS-NCM half-cells, this electrode delivers a large reversible capacity of 180 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, a good cycling stability, capacity, and a high-rate capability. In addition, the full-cells coupling LS-NCM as the cathode with hard carbon as the anode exhibit a reversible capacity of 100 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, long-term cycle-life and high power properties. The rational design of the layered P3/P2 and spinel intergrowth composite offers a new insight into enhancing the cycle-life and rate capability of sodium transition-metal oxide cathodes for SIBs.</span>

            <br><a href="https://cookiepedia.co.uk/giving-consent-to-cookies" class="privacy-notice-link" rel="noopener" target="_blank" aria-label="More information about your privacy, opens in a new tab">More information</a></div><button id="accept-recommended-btn-handler">Allow all</button><section class="ot-sdk-row ot-cat-grp"><h3 id="ot-category-title"> Manage consent preferences</h3><div class="ot-pli-hdr ot-leg-border-color"><span class="ot-li-title">Consent</span> <span class="ot-li-title">Legitimate interest</span></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0001"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0001" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0001 ot-status-id-C0001"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0001">Strictly necessary cookies</h4></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0001">These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation. They may be set by us or by third party providers whose services we have added to our pages. If you do not allow these cookies then some or all of these services may not function properly.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0004"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0004">Targeting cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0004" id="ot-group-id-C0004" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0004" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0004"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0004"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Targeting cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0004">These cookies may be set through our site by our advertising partners.
